<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Free will</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Ability to make choices without constraints</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Free_will_(disambiguation)" title="Free will (disambiguation)">Free will (disambiguation)</a>. "Freewill" redirects here; for the software company, see <a href="/wiki/FreeWill" title="FreeWill">FreeWill</a>.</div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Self-agency" title="Self-agency">Self-agency</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abm2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Abm2.jpg/260px-Abm2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Abm2.jpg/390px-Abm2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Abm2.jpg/520px-Abm2.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abm2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A person is jumping into the water, which can be considered as a result of free will.</div></div></div>
<p><b>Free will</b> is the capacity of agents to <a href="/wiki/Choice" title="Choice">choose</a> between different possible courses of <a href="/wiki/Action_(philosophy)" title="Action (philosophy)">action</a> unimpeded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carus1910_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carus1910-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Free will is closely linked to the concepts of <a href="/wiki/Moral_responsibility" title="Moral responsibility">moral responsibility</a>, <a href="/wiki/Praise" title="Praise">praise</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culpability" title="Culpability">culpability</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sin" title="Sin">sin</a>, and other judgements which apply only to actions that are freely chosen. It is also connected with the concepts of <a href="/wiki/Advice_(opinion)" title="Advice (opinion)">advice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persuasion" title="Persuasion">persuasion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deliberation" title="Deliberation">deliberation</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Prohibitionism" title="Prohibitionism">prohibition</a>. Traditionally, only actions that are freely <a href="/wiki/Will_(philosophy)" title="Will (philosophy)">willed</a> are seen as deserving credit or blame. Whether free will exists, what it is and the implications of whether it exists or not are some of the longest running debates of philosophy and religion. Some conceive of free will as the right to act outside of external influences or wishes.
</p><p>Some conceive free will to be the capacity to make choices undetermined by past events. <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">Determinism</a> suggests that only one course of events is possible, which is inconsistent with a libertarian model of free will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baumeister,_R.F_2014._pp._1-52_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baumeister,_R.F_2014._pp._1-52-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Ancient Greek philosophy</a> identified this issue,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bobzien1998determinism_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-bobzien1998determinism-4">[4]</a></sup> which remains a major focus of philosophical debate. The view that conceives free will as incompatible with determinism is called <i><a href="/wiki/Incompatibilism" title="Incompatibilism">incompatibilism</a></i> and encompasses both <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism_(metaphysics)" title="Libertarianism (metaphysics)">metaphysical libertarianism</a> (the claim that determinism is false and thus free will is at least possible) and <a href="/wiki/Hard_determinism" title="Hard determinism">hard determinism</a> (the claim that determinism is true and thus free will is not possible). Incompatibilism also encompasses <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hard_incompatibilism" title="Hard incompatibilism">hard incompatibilism</a>, which holds not only determinism but also <a href="/wiki/Indeterminism" title="Indeterminism">indeterminism</a> to be incompatible with free will and thus free will to be impossible whatever the case may be regarding determinism.
</p><p>In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Compatibilism" title="Compatibilism">compatibilists</a> hold that free will <i>is</i> compatible with determinism. Some compatibilists even hold that determinism is <i>necessary</i> for free will, arguing that choice involves preference for one course of action over another, requiring a sense of <i>how</i> choices will turn out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carnap_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carnap-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bishop_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bishop-6">[6]</a></sup> Compatibilists thus consider the debate between libertarians and hard determinists over free will vs. determinism a <a href="/wiki/False_dilemma" title="False dilemma">false dilemma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richards_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richards-7">[7]</a></sup> Different compatibilists offer very different definitions of what "free will" means and consequently find different types of constraints to be relevant to the issue. Classical compatibilists considered free will nothing more than freedom of action, considering one free of will simply if, <i>had</i> one counterfactually wanted to do otherwise, one <i>could</i> have done otherwise without physical impediment. Contemporary compatibilists instead identify free will as a psychological capacity, such as to direct one's behavior in a way responsive to reason, and there are still further different conceptions of free will, each with their own concerns, sharing only the common feature of not finding the possibility of determinism a threat to the possibility of free will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_free_will">History of free will</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History of free will">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The problem of free will has been identified in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">ancient Greek philosophical</a> literature. The notion of compatibilist free will has been attributed to both <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> (fourth century BCE) and <a href="/wiki/Epictetus" title="Epictetus">Epictetus</a> (1st century CE); "it was the fact that nothing hindered us from doing or choosing something that made us have control over them".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bobzien1998determinism_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-bobzien1998determinism-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Susanne_Bobzien" title="Susanne Bobzien">Susanne Bobzien</a>, the notion of incompatibilist free will is perhaps first identified in the works of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_of_Aphrodisias" title="Alexander of Aphrodisias">Alexander of Aphrodisias</a> (third century CE); "what makes us have control over things is the fact that we are causally undetermined in our decision and thus can freely decide between doing/choosing or not doing/choosing them".
</p><p>The term "free will" (<i>liberum arbitrium</i>) was introduced by Christian philosophy (4th century CE). It has traditionally meant (until the Enlightenment proposed its own meanings) lack of necessity in human will,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> so that "the will is free" meant "the will does not have to be such as it is". This requirement was universally embraced by both incompatibilists and compatibilists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_philosophy">Western philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Western philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Free_will_in_antiquity" title="Free will in antiquity">Free will in antiquity</a></div>
<p>The underlying questions are whether we have control over our actions, and if so, what sort of control, and to what extent. These questions predate the early Greek <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">stoics</a> (for example, <a href="/wiki/Chrysippus" title="Chrysippus">Chrysippus</a>), and some modern philosophers lament the lack of progress over all these centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nagel_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nagel-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Searle_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Searle-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>On one hand, humans have a strong sense of freedom, which leads them to believe that they have free will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> On the other hand, an intuitive feeling of free will could be mistaken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baumeister2_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baumeister2-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clark_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is difficult to reconcile the intuitive evidence that conscious decisions are causally effective with the view that the physical world can be explained entirely by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_law" title="Physical law">physical law</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Velmans2002_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Velmans2002-18">[18]</a></sup> The conflict between intuitively felt freedom and natural law arises when either <a href="/wiki/Causal_closure" title="Causal closure">causal closure</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_determinism" title="Physical determinism">physical determinism</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nomological_determinism" title="Nomological determinism">nomological determinism</a>) is asserted. With causal closure, no physical event has a cause outside the physical domain, and with physical determinism, the future is determined entirely by preceding events (cause and effect).
</p><p>The puzzle of reconciling 'free will' with a deterministic universe is known as the <i>problem of free will</i> or sometimes referred to as the <i>dilemma of determinism</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WJames_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-WJames-19">[19]</a></sup> This dilemma leads to a <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">moral</a> dilemma as well: the question of how to assign <a href="/wiki/Moral_responsibility" title="Moral responsibility">responsibility</a> for actions if they are caused entirely by past events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bargh_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bargh-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russell-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Compatibilists maintain that mental reality is not of itself causally effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shariff_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shariff-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VelmansM_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-VelmansM-23">[23]</a></sup> Classical <a href="/wiki/Compatibilism" title="Compatibilism">compatibilists</a> have addressed the dilemma of free will by arguing that free will holds as long as humans are not externally constrained or coerced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strawson_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-strawson-24">[24]</a></sup> Modern compatibilists make a distinction between freedom of will and freedom of <i>action</i>, that is, separating <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_choice" title="Freedom of choice">freedom of choice</a> from the freedom to enact it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OConnor_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-OConnor-25">[25]</a></sup> Given that humans all experience a sense of free will, some modern compatibilists think it is necessary to accommodate this intuition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greene_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greene-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman1_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman1-27">[27]</a></sup> Compatibilists often associate freedom of will with the <a href="/wiki/Ability" title="Ability">ability</a> to make rational decisions.
</p><p>A different approach to the dilemma is that of <a href="/wiki/Incompatibilism" title="Incompatibilism">incompatibilists</a>, namely, that if the world is deterministic, then our feeling that we are free to choose an action is simply an <a href="/wiki/Hard_determinism" title="Hard determinism">illusion</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metaphysical_libertarianism" title="Metaphysical libertarianism">Metaphysical libertarianism</a> is the form of incompatibilism which posits that <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">determinism</a> is false and free will is possible (at least some people have free will).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordcompatibilism_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordcompatibilism-28">[28]</a></sup> This view is associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">non-materialist</a> constructions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baumeister2_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baumeister2-16">[16]</a></sup> including both traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)#Substance_or_Cartesian_dualism" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">dualism</a>, as well as models supporting more minimal criteria; such as the ability to consciously veto an action or competing desire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Libet_2003_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Libet_2003-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RKane1_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-RKane1-30">[30]</a></sup> Yet even with <a href="/wiki/Indeterminism" title="Indeterminism">physical indeterminism</a>, arguments have been made against libertarianism in that it is difficult to assign <i>Origination</i> (responsibility for "free" indeterministic choices).
</p><p>Free will here is predominantly treated with respect to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_determinism" title="Physical determinism">physical determinism</a> in the strict sense of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nomological_determinism" title="Nomological determinism">nomological determinism</a>, although other forms of determinism are also relevant to free will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordincompatibilismarguments_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordincompatibilismarguments-31">[31]</a></sup> For example, logical and <a href="/wiki/Theological_determinism" title="Theological determinism">theological determinism</a> challenge metaphysical libertarianism with ideas of <a href="/wiki/Destiny" title="Destiny">destiny</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fate" title="Fate">fate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biological_determinism" title="Biological determinism">biological</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cultural_determinism" title="Cultural determinism">cultural</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychological_determinism" title="Psychological determinism">psychological</a> determinism feed the development of compatibilist models. Separate classes of <a href="/wiki/Compatibilism" title="Compatibilism">compatibilism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Incompatibilism" title="Incompatibilism">incompatibilism</a> may even be formed to represent these.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordforeknowledge_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordforeknowledge-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Below are the classic arguments bearing upon the dilemma and its underpinnings.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Incompatibilism">Incompatibilism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Incompatibilism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Incompatibilism" title="Incompatibilism">Incompatibilism</a></div>
<p>Incompatibilism is the position that free will and determinism are logically incompatible, and that the major question regarding whether or not people have free will is thus whether or not their actions are determined. "Hard determinists", such as <a href="/wiki/Baron_d%27Holbach" title="Baron d'Holbach">d'Holbach</a>, are those incompatibilists who accept determinism and reject free will. In contrast, "<a href="/wiki/Libertarianism_(metaphysics)" title="Libertarianism (metaphysics)">metaphysical libertarians</a>", such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Reid" title="Thomas Reid">Thomas Reid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_van_Inwagen" title="Peter van Inwagen">Peter van Inwagen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Kane_(philosopher)" title="Robert Kane (philosopher)">Robert Kane</a>, are those incompatibilists who accept free will and deny determinism, holding the view that some form of indeterminism is true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wagen_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wagen-33">[33]</a></sup> Another view is that of hard incompatibilists, which state that free will is incompatible with both <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">determinism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indeterminism" title="Indeterminism">indeterminism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Derk1_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Derk1-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditional arguments for incompatibilism are based on an "<a href="/wiki/Intuition_pump" title="Intuition pump">intuition pump</a>": if a person is like other mechanical things that are determined in their behavior such as a wind-up toy, a billiard ball, a puppet, or a robot, then people must not have free will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wagen_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wagen-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> This argument has been rejected by compatibilists such as <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a> on the grounds that, even if humans have something in common with these things, it remains possible and plausible that we are different from such objects in important ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DD1_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-DD1-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another argument for incompatibilism is that of the "causal chain". Incompatibilism is key to the idealist theory of free will. Most incompatibilists reject the idea that freedom of action consists simply in "voluntary" behavior. They insist, rather, that free will means that someone must be the "ultimate" or "originating" cause of his actions. They must be <i><a href="/wiki/Causa_sui" title="Causa sui">causa sui</a></i>, in the traditional phrase. Being responsible for one's choices is the first cause of those choices, where first cause means that there is no antecedent cause of that cause. The argument, then, is that if a person has free will, then they are the ultimate cause of their actions. If determinism is true, then all of a person's choices are caused by events and facts outside their control. So, if everything someone does is caused by events and facts outside their control, then they cannot be the ultimate cause of their actions. Therefore, they cannot have free will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> This argument has also been challenged by various compatibilist philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>A third argument for incompatibilism was formulated by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Ginet" title="Carl Ginet">Carl Ginet</a> in the 1960s and has received much attention in the modern literature. The simplified argument runs along these lines: if determinism is true, then we have no control over the events of the past that determined our present state and no control over the laws of nature. Since we can have no control over these matters, we also can have no control over the <i>consequences</i> of them. Since our present choices and acts, under determinism, are the necessary consequences of the past and the laws of nature, then we have no control over them and, hence, no free will. This is called the <i>consequence argument</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ving_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ving-43">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peter_van_Inwagen" title="Peter van Inwagen">Peter van Inwagen</a> remarks that C.D. Broad had a version of the consequence argument as early as the 1930s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>The difficulty of this argument for some compatibilists lies in the fact that it entails the impossibility that one could have chosen other than one has. For example, if Jane is a compatibilist and she has just sat down on the sofa, then she is committed to the claim that she could have remained standing, if she had so desired. But it <a href="/wiki/Logical_consequence" title="Logical consequence">follows from</a> the consequence argument that, if Jane had remained standing, she would have either generated a contradiction, violated the laws of nature or changed the past. Hence, compatibilists are committed to the existence of "incredible abilities", according to Ginet and van Inwagen. One response to this argument is that it equivocates on the notions of abilities and necessities, or that the free will evoked to make any given choice is really an illusion and the choice had been made all along, oblivious to its "decider".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ving_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ving-43">[43]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Kellogg_Lewis" title="David Kellogg Lewis">David Lewis</a> suggests that compatibilists are only committed to the ability to do something otherwise if <i>different circumstances</i> had actually obtained in the past.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Using <i>T</i>, <i>F</i> for "true" and "false" and <i>?</i> for undecided, there are exactly nine positions regarding determinism/free will that consist of any two of these three possibilities:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strawson_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strawson-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; width:50%;">

<p><i>Incompatibilism</i> may occupy any of the nine positions except (5), (8) or (3), which last corresponds to <i>soft determinism</i>. Position (1) is <i>hard determinism</i>, and position (2) is <i>libertarianism</i>. The position (1) of hard determinism adds to the table the contention that <i>D</i> implies <i>FW</i> is untrue, and the position (2) of libertarianism adds the contention that <i>FW</i> implies <i>D</i> is untrue. Position (9) may be called <i>hard incompatibilism</i> if one interprets <i>?</i> as meaning both concepts are of dubious value. <i>Compatibilism</i> itself may occupy any of the nine positions, that is, there is no logical contradiction between determinism and free will, and either or both may be true or false in principle. However, the most common meaning attached to <i>compatibilism</i> is that some form of determinism is true and yet we have some form of free will, position (3).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FischerFour2009_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FischerFour2009-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Toppledominos.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="680" data-file-width="1023" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Toppledominos.jpg/220px-Toppledominos.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Toppledominos.jpg/330px-Toppledominos.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Toppledominos.jpg/440px-Toppledominos.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Toppledominos.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Domino_effect" title="Domino effect">domino's movement</a> is <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">determined</a> completely by laws of physics.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Rosenberg" title="Alexander Rosenberg">Alex Rosenberg</a> makes an extrapolation of physical determinism as inferred on the macroscopic scale by the behaviour of a set of dominoes to neural activity in the brain where; "If the brain is nothing but a complex physical object whose states are as much governed by physical laws as any other physical object, then what goes on in our heads is as fixed and determined by prior events as what goes on when one domino topples another in a long row of them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosenberg_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenberg-48">[48]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Determinism#Quantum_mechanics_and_classical_physics" title="Determinism">Physical determinism</a> is currently disputed by prominent <a href="/wiki/Interpretations_of_quantum_mechanics" title="Interpretations of quantum mechanics">interpretations of quantum mechanics</a>, and while not necessarily representative of intrinsic <a href="/wiki/Indeterminism" title="Indeterminism">indeterminism</a> in nature, fundamental limits of precision in measurement are inherent in the <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty_principle" title="Uncertainty principle">uncertainty principle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bohr1_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bohr1-49">[49]</a></sup> The relevance of such prospective indeterminate activity to free will is, however, contested,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bohr_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bohr-50">[50]</a></sup> even when chaos theory is introduced to magnify the effects of such microscopic events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RKane1_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-RKane1-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Below these positions are examined in more detail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strawson_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-Strawson-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hard_determinism">Hard determinism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Hard determinism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hard_determinism" title="Hard determinism">Hard determinism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FreeWillTaxonomy4.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="440" data-file-width="690" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/FreeWillTaxonomy4.svg/220px-FreeWillTaxonomy4.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/FreeWillTaxonomy4.svg/330px-FreeWillTaxonomy4.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/FreeWillTaxonomy4.svg/440px-FreeWillTaxonomy4.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FreeWillTaxonomy4.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A simplified <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(general)" title="Taxonomy (general)">taxonomy</a> of philosophical positions regarding free will and determinism.</div></div></div>
<p>Determinism can be divided into causal, logical and theological determinism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parkinson2012_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parkinson2012-52">[52]</a></sup> Corresponding to each of these different meanings, there arises a different problem for free will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Viv_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Viv-53">[53]</a></sup> Hard determinism is the claim that <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">determinism</a> is true, and that it is <a href="/wiki/Incompatibilism" title="Incompatibilism">incompatible with free will</a>, so free will does not exist. Although hard determinism generally refers to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nomological_determinism" title="Nomological determinism">nomological determinism</a> (see causal determinism below), it can include all forms of determinism that necessitate the future in its entirety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanArragon2010_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-VanArragon2010-54">[54]</a></sup> Relevant forms of determinism include:
</p>
<dl><dt><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Causal_determinism" title="Causal determinism">Causal determinism</a></dt>
<dd>The idea that everything is caused by prior conditions, making it impossible for anything else to happen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordmoralresponsibility_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordmoralresponsibility-55">[55]</a></sup> In its most common form, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nomological_determinism" title="Nomological determinism">nomological (or scientific) determinism</a>, future events are necessitated by past and present events combined with the laws of nature. Such determinism is sometimes illustrated by the <a href="/wiki/Thought_experiment" title="Thought experiment">thought experiment</a> of <a href="/wiki/Laplace%27s_demon" title="Laplace's demon">Laplace's demon</a>. Imagine an entity that knows all facts about the past and the present, and knows all natural laws that govern the universe. If the laws of nature were determinate, then such an entity would be able to use this knowledge to foresee the future, down to the smallest detail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-determinism_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-determinism-57">[57]</a></sup></dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Logical_determinism" title="Logical determinism">Logical determinism</a></dt>
<dd>The notion that all <a href="/wiki/Proposition" title="Proposition">propositions</a>, whether about the past, present or future, are either true or false. The problem of free will, in this context, is the problem of how choices can be free, given that what one does in the future is already determined as true or false in the present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Viv_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Viv-53">[53]</a></sup></dd>
<dt><a href="/wiki/Theological_determinism" title="Theological determinism">Theological determinism</a></dt>
<dd>The idea that the future is already determined, either by a <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">creator deity</a> decreeing or <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">knowing</a> its outcome in advance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FischerGod1989_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FischerGod1989-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watt_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watt-59">[59]</a></sup> The problem of free will, in this context, is the problem of how our actions can be free if there is a being who has determined them for us in advance, or if they are already set in time.</dd></dl>
<p>Other forms of determinism are more relevant to compatibilism, such as <a href="/wiki/Biological_determinism" title="Biological determinism">biological determinism</a>, the idea that all behaviors, beliefs, and desires are fixed by our genetic endowment and our biochemical makeup, the latter of which is affected by both genes and environment, <a href="/wiki/Cultural_determinism" title="Cultural determinism">cultural determinism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychological_determinism" title="Psychological determinism">psychological determinism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Viv_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Viv-53">[53]</a></sup> Combinations and syntheses of determinist theses, such as bio-environmental determinism, are even more common.
</p><p>Suggestions have been made that hard determinism need not maintain strict determinism, where something near to, like that informally known as <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">adequate determinism</a>, is perhaps more relevant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordincompatibilismarguments_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordincompatibilismarguments-31">[31]</a></sup> Despite this, hard determinism has grown less popular in present times, given scientific suggestions that determinism is false – yet the intention of their position is sustained by hard incompatibilism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordcompatibilism_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordcompatibilism-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Metaphysical_libertarianism">Metaphysical libertarianism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Metaphysical libertarianism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism_(metaphysics)" title="Libertarianism (metaphysics)">Libertarianism (metaphysics)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FreewillConceptsBasedUponPhilosophyOfMindCausationViews.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="569" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/FreewillConceptsBasedUponPhilosophyOfMindCausationViews.svg/220px-FreewillConceptsBasedUponPhilosophyOfMindCausationViews.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/FreewillConceptsBasedUponPhilosophyOfMindCausationViews.svg/330px-FreewillConceptsBasedUponPhilosophyOfMindCausationViews.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/FreewillConceptsBasedUponPhilosophyOfMindCausationViews.svg/440px-FreewillConceptsBasedUponPhilosophyOfMindCausationViews.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FreewillConceptsBasedUponPhilosophyOfMindCausationViews.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Various definitions of free will that have been proposed for Metaphysical Libertarianism (agent/substance causal,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordincompatibilismtheories_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordincompatibilismtheories-60">[60]</a></sup> centered accounts,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kane2005_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kane2005-61">[61]</a></sup> and efforts of will theory<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RKane1_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-RKane1-30">[30]</a></sup>), along with examples of other common free will positions (Compatibilism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Velmans2002_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Velmans2002-18">[18]</a></sup> Hard Determinism,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> and Hard Incompatibilism<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Derk1_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Derk1-34">[34]</a></sup>). Red circles represent mental states; blue circles represent physical states; arrows describe causal interaction.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Libertarianism_(metaphysics)" title="Libertarianism (metaphysics)">Metaphysical libertarianism</a> is one philosophical view point under that of incompatibilism. Libertarianism holds onto a concept of free will that requires that the <a href="/wiki/Agency_(philosophy)" title="Agency (philosophy)">agent</a> be able to take more than one possible course of action under a given set of circumstances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georgiev-2021_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Georgiev-2021-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>Accounts of libertarianism subdivide into non-physical theories and physical or naturalistic theories. Non-physical theories hold that the events in the brain that lead to the performance of actions do not have an entirely physical explanation, which requires that the world is not closed under physics. This includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interactionist_dualism" title="Interactionist dualism">interactionist dualism</a>, which claims that some non-physical <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a>, will, or <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a> overrides physical <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">causality</a>. Physical determinism implies there is only one possible future and is therefore not compatible with libertarian free will. As consequent of incompatibilism, metaphysical libertarian explanations that do not involve dispensing with <a href="/wiki/Physicalism" title="Physicalism">physicalism</a> require physical indeterminism, such as probabilistic subatomic particle behavior – theory unknown to many of the early writers on free will. Incompatibilist theories can be categorised based on the type of indeterminism they require; uncaused events, non-deterministically caused events, and agent/substance-caused events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordincompatibilismtheories_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordincompatibilismtheories-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-causal_theories">Non-causal theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Non-causal theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Non-causal accounts of incompatibilist free will do not require a free action to be caused by either an agent or a physical event. They either rely upon a world that is not causally closed, or physical indeterminism. Non-causal accounts often claim that each intentional action requires a choice or volition – a willing, trying, or endeavoring on behalf of the agent (such as the cognitive component of lifting one's arm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LumerNannini2007_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-LumerNannini2007-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCann1998_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCann1998-65">[65]</a></sup> Such intentional actions are interpreted as free actions. It has been suggested, however, that such acting cannot be said to exercise control over anything in particular. According to non-causal accounts, the causation by the agent cannot be analysed in terms of causation by mental states or events, including desire, belief, intention of something in particular, but rather is considered a matter of spontaneity and creativity. The exercise of intent in such intentional actions is not that which determines their freedom – intentional actions are rather self-generating. The "actish feel" of some intentional actions do not "constitute that event's activeness, or the agent's exercise of active control", rather they "might be brought about by direct stimulation of someone's brain, in the absence of any relevant desire or intention on the part of that person".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordincompatibilismtheories_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordincompatibilismtheories-60">[60]</a></sup> Another question raised by such non-causal theory, is how an agent acts upon reason, if the said intentional actions are spontaneous.
</p><p>Some non-causal explanations involve invoking <a href="/wiki/Panpsychism" title="Panpsychism">panpsychism</a>, the theory that a quality of <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> is associated with all particles, and pervades the entire universe, in both animate and inanimate entities.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Event-causal_theories">Event-causal theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Event-causal theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Event-causal accounts of incompatibilist free will typically rely upon physicalist models of mind (like those of the compatibilist), yet they presuppose physical indeterminism, in which certain indeterministic events are said to be caused by the agent. A number of event-causal accounts of free will have been created, referenced here as <i>deliberative indeterminism</i>, <i>centred accounts</i>, and <i>efforts of will theory</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordincompatibilismtheories_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordincompatibilismtheories-60">[60]</a></sup> The first two accounts do not require free will to be a fundamental constituent of the universe. Ordinary randomness is appealed to as supplying the "elbow room" that libertarians believe necessary. A first common objection to event-causal accounts is that the indeterminism could be destructive and could therefore diminish control by the agent rather than provide it (related to the problem of origination). A second common objection to these models is that it is questionable whether such indeterminism could add any value to deliberation over that which is already present in a deterministic world.
</p><p><i>Deliberative indeterminism</i> asserts that the indeterminism is confined to an earlier stage in the decision process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ekstrom2000_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ekstrom2000-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mele2006_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mele2006-67">[67]</a></sup> This is intended to provide an indeterminate set of possibilities to choose from, while not risking the introduction of <i>luck</i> (random decision making). The selection process is deterministic, although it may be based on earlier preferences established by the same process. Deliberative indeterminism has been referenced by <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dennett1981_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dennett1981-68">[68]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/John_Martin_Fischer" title="John Martin Fischer">John Martin Fischer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-L._PetersonFischer1995_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-L._PetersonFischer1995-69">[69]</a></sup> An obvious objection to such a view is that an agent cannot be assigned ownership over their decisions (or preferences used to make those decisions) to any greater degree than that of a compatibilist model.
</p><p><i>Centred accounts</i> propose that for any given decision between two possibilities, the strength of reason will be considered for each option, yet there is still a probability the weaker candidate will be chosen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kane2005_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kane2005-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balaguer1999_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Balaguer1999-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nozick1981_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nozick1981-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorabji1980_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sorabji1980-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inwagen1983_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Inwagen1983-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wiggins1973_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wiggins1973-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Searle2001_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Searle2001-75">[75]</a></sup> An obvious objection to such a view is that decisions are explicitly left up to chance, and origination or responsibility cannot be assigned for any given decision.
</p><p><i>Efforts of will theory</i> is related to the role of will power in decision making. It suggests that the indeterminacy of agent volition processes could map to the indeterminacy of certain physical events – and the outcomes of these events could therefore be considered caused by the agent. Models of <a href="/wiki/Volition_(psychology)" title="Volition (psychology)">volition</a> have been constructed in which it is seen as a particular kind of complex, high-level process with an element of physical indeterminism. An example of this approach is that of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Kane_(philosopher)" title="Robert Kane (philosopher)">Robert Kane</a>, where he hypothesizes that "in each case, the indeterminism is functioning as a hindrance or obstacle to her realizing one of her purposes – a hindrance or obstacle in the form of resistance within her will which must be overcome by effort."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RKane1_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-RKane1-30">[30]</a></sup> According to Robert Kane such "ultimate responsibility" is a required condition for free will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kane1996_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kane1996-76">[76]</a></sup> An important factor in such a theory is that the agent cannot be reduced to physical neuronal events, but rather mental processes are said to provide an equally valid account of the determination of outcome as their physical processes (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-reductive_physicalism" title="Non-reductive physicalism">non-reductive physicalism</a>).
</p><p>Although at the time <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> (and physical <a href="/wiki/Quantum_indeterminacy" title="Quantum indeterminacy">indeterminism</a>) was only in the initial stages of acceptance, in his book <a href="/wiki/Miracles_(book)" title="Miracles (book)"><i>Miracles: A preliminary study</i></a> C.S. Lewis stated the logical possibility that if the physical world were proved indeterministic this would provide an entry point to describe an action of a non-physical entity on physical reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSLewis1_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-CSLewis1-77">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Indeterminism" title="Indeterminism">Indeterministic</a> physical models (particularly those involving <a href="/wiki/Quantum_indeterminacy" title="Quantum indeterminacy">quantum indeterminacy</a>) introduce random occurrences at an atomic or subatomic level. These events might affect brain activity, and could seemingly allow <a href="/wiki/Incompatibilism" title="Incompatibilism">incompatibilist</a> free will if the apparent indeterminacy of some mental processes (for instance, subjective perceptions of control in conscious <a href="/wiki/Volition_(psychology)" title="Volition (psychology)">volition</a>) map to the underlying indeterminacy of the physical construct. This relationship, however, requires a causative role over probabilities that is questionable,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-quantum_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-quantum-78">[78]</a></sup> and it is far from established that brain activity responsible for human action can be affected by such events. Secondarily, these incompatibilist models are dependent upon the relationship between action and conscious volition, as studied in the <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience_of_free_will" title="Neuroscience of free will">neuroscience of free will</a>. It is evident that observation may disturb the outcome of the observation itself, rendering limited our ability to identify causality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bohr1_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bohr1-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Niels_Bohr" title="Niels Bohr">Niels Bohr</a>, one of the main architects of quantum theory, suggested, however, that no connection could be made between indeterminism of nature and freedom of will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bohr_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bohr-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Agent.2Fsubstance-causal_theories"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Agent/substance-causal_theories">Agent/substance-causal theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Agent/substance-causal theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Agent/substance-causal accounts of incompatibilist free will rely upon substance dualism in their description of mind. The agent is assumed power to intervene in the physical world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chisholm2004_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chisholm2004-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-larke1996_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-larke1996-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Donagan1987_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Donagan1987-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OConner2002_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-OConner2002-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rowe1991_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rowe1991-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor1966_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor1966-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thorp1980_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thorp1980-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zimmerman1984_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zimmerman1984-86">[86]</a></sup>
Agent (substance)-causal accounts have been suggested by both <a href="/wiki/George_Berkeley" title="George Berkeley">George Berkeley</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BerkeleyDancy1998_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-BerkeleyDancy1998-87">[87]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Reid" title="Thomas Reid">Thomas Reid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reid2012_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reid2012-88">[88]</a></sup> It is required that what the agent causes is not causally determined by prior events. It is also required that the agent's causing of that event is not causally determined by prior events. A number of problems have been identified with this view. Firstly, it is difficult to establish the reason for any given choice by the agent, which suggests they may be random or determined by <i>luck</i> (without an underlying basis for the free will decision). Secondly, it has been questioned whether physical events can be caused by an external substance or mind – a common problem associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">interactionalist dualism</a>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hard_incompatibilism">Hard incompatibilism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Hard incompatibilism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Hard incompatibilism is the idea that free will cannot exist, whether the world is deterministic or not. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Derk_Pereboom_(philosopher)" title="Derk Pereboom (philosopher)">Derk Pereboom</a> has defended hard incompatibilism, identifying a variety of positions where free will is irrelevant to indeterminism/determinism, among them the following:
</p>
<dl><dd><ol><li>Determinism (D) is true, D does not imply we lack free will (F), but in fact we do lack F.</li>
<li>D is true, D does not imply we lack F, but in fact we don't know if we have F.</li>
<li>D is true, and we do have F.</li>
<li>D is true, we have F, and F implies D.</li>
<li>D is unproven, but we have F.</li>
<li>D isn't true, we do have F, and would have F even if D were true.</li>
<li>D isn't true, we don't have F, but F is compatible with D.</li></ol>
<dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd>Derk Pereboom, <i>Living without Free Will</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Derk1_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-Derk1-34">[34]</a></sup> p. xvi.</dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<p>Pereboom calls positions 3 and 4 <i>soft determinism</i>, position 1 a form of <i>hard determinism</i>, position 6 a form of <i>classical libertarianism</i>, and any position that includes having F as <i>compatibilism</i>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a> denied that the phrase "free will" made any sense (compare with <a href="/wiki/Theological_noncognitivism" title="Theological noncognitivism">theological noncognitivism</a>, a similar stance on the <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">existence of God</a>). He also took the view that the truth of determinism was irrelevant. He believed that the defining feature of voluntary behavior was that individuals have the ability to <i>postpone</i> a decision long enough to reflect or deliberate upon the consequences of a choice: "…the will in truth, signifies nothing but a power, or ability, to prefer or choose".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>The contemporary philosopher <a href="/wiki/Galen_Strawson" title="Galen Strawson">Galen Strawson</a> agrees with Locke that the truth or falsity of determinism is irrelevant to the
problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GStraw_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-GStraw-90">[90]</a></sup> He argues that the notion of free will leads to an infinite regress and is therefore senseless.
According to Strawson, if one is responsible for what one does in a given situation, then one must be responsible for the way one is in certain mental respects. But it is impossible for one to be responsible for the way one is in any respect. This is because to be responsible in some situation <i>S</i>, one must have been responsible for the way one was at <i>S<sup>−1</sup></i>. To be responsible for the way one was at <i>S<sup>−1</sup></i>, one must have been responsible for the way one was at <i>S<sup>−2</sup></i>, and so on. At some point in the chain, there must have been an act of origination of a new causal chain. But this is impossible. Man cannot create himself or his mental states <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ex_nihilo" title="Ex nihilo">ex nihilo</a></i>. This argument entails that free will itself is absurd, but not that it is incompatible with determinism. Strawson calls his own view "pessimism" but it can be classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hard_incompatibilism" title="Hard incompatibilism">hard incompatibilism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GStraw_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-GStraw-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Causal_determinism">Causal determinism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Causal determinism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">Determinism</a></div>
<p>Causal determinism is the concept that <a href="/wiki/Event_(philosophy)" title="Event (philosophy)">events</a> within a given <a href="/wiki/Paradigm" title="Paradigm">paradigm</a> are bound by <a href="/wiki/Causality" title="Causality">causality</a> in such a way that any state (of an object or event) is completely determined by prior states. Causal determinism proposes that there is an unbroken chain of prior occurrences stretching back to the origin of the universe. Causal determinists believe that there is nothing uncaused or <a href="/wiki/Causa_sui" title="Causa sui">self-caused</a>. The most common form of causal determinism is nomological determinism (or scientific determinism), the notion that the past and the present dictate the future entirely and necessarily by rigid natural laws, that every occurrence results inevitably from prior events. <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">Quantum mechanics</a> poses a serious challenge to this view.
</p><p>Fundamental debate continues over whether the physical universe is likely to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Causal_determinism" title="Causal determinism">deterministic</a>. Although the scientific method cannot be used to rule out <a href="/wiki/Indeterminism" title="Indeterminism">indeterminism</a> with respect to violations of <a href="/wiki/Causal_closure" title="Causal closure">causal closure</a>, it can be used to identify indeterminism in natural law. <a href="/wiki/Interpretations_of_quantum_mechanics" title="Interpretations of quantum mechanics">Interpretations of quantum mechanics</a> at present are both <a href="/wiki/Deterministic_system" title="Deterministic system">deterministic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indeterminism" title="Indeterminism">indeterministic</a>, and are being constrained by ongoing experimentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GroblacherPaterek2007_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-GroblacherPaterek2007-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Destiny_and_fate">Destiny and fate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Destiny and fate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Destiny" title="Destiny">Destiny</a></div>
<p>Destiny or fate is a predetermined course of events. It may be conceived as a predetermined future, whether in general or of an individual. It is a concept based on the belief that there is a fixed natural order to the cosmos.
</p><p>Although often used interchangeably, the words "fate" and "destiny" have distinct connotations.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fate" title="Fate">Fate</a> generally implies there is a set course that cannot be deviated from, and over which one has no control. Fate is related to <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">determinism</a>, but makes no specific claim of physical determinism. Even with physical indeterminism an event could still be fated externally (see for instance <a href="/wiki/Theological_determinism" title="Theological determinism">theological determinism</a>). Destiny likewise is related to determinism, but makes no specific claim of physical determinism. Even with physical indeterminism an event could still be destined to occur.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Destiny" title="Destiny">Destiny</a> implies there is a set course that cannot be deviated from, but does not of itself make any claim with respect to the setting of that course (i.e., it does not necessarily conflict with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incompatibilist" title="Incompatibilist">incompatibilist</a> free will). Free will if existent could be the mechanism by which that destined outcome is chosen (determined to represent destiny).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blackwell2011_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blackwell2011-92">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Logical_determinism">Logical determinism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Logical determinism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/B-theory_of_time" title="B-theory of time">B-theory of time</a></div>
<p>Discussion regarding destiny does not necessitate the existence of supernatural powers. Logical <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">determinism</a> or determinateness is the notion that all propositions, whether about the past, present, or future, are either true or false. This creates a unique problem for free will given that propositions about the future already have a truth value in the present (that is it is already determined as either true or false), and is referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_future_contingents" title="Problem of future contingents">problem of future contingents</a>.
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Omniscience">Omniscience</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Omniscience">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">Omniscience</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">Omniscience</a> is the capacity to know everything that there is to know (included in which are all future events), and is a property often attributed to a creator deity. Omniscience implies the existence of destiny. Some authors have claimed that free will cannot coexist with omniscience. One argument asserts that an omniscient creator not only implies destiny but a form of high level <a href="/wiki/Predeterminism" title="Predeterminism">predeterminism</a> such as hard <a href="/wiki/Theological_determinism" title="Theological determinism">theological determinism</a> or <a href="/wiki/Predestination" title="Predestination">predestination</a> – that they have independently fixed all events and outcomes in the universe in advance. In such a case, even if an individual could have influence over their lower level physical system, their choices in regard to this cannot be their own, as is the case with libertarian free will. Omniscience features as an <a href="/wiki/Incompatible-properties_argument" title="Incompatible-properties argument">incompatible-properties argument</a> for the existence of <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>, known as the <a href="/wiki/Argument_from_free_will" title="Argument from free will">argument from free will</a>, and is closely related to other such arguments, for example the incompatibility of <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">omnipotence</a> with a good creator deity (i.e. if a deity knew what they were going to choose, then they are responsible for letting them choose it).
</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Predeterminism">Predeterminism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Predeterminism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Predeterminism" title="Predeterminism">Predeterminism</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Predestination" title="Predestination">Predestination</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Predeterminism" title="Predeterminism">Predeterminism</a> is the idea that all events are determined in advance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKewan_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-McKewan-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Predeterminism is the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> that all events of <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a>, past, present and future, have been decided or are known (by <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fate" title="Fate">fate</a>, or some other force), including human actions. Predeterminism is frequently taken to mean that human actions cannot interfere with (or have no bearing on) the outcomes of a pre-determined course of events, and that one's destiny was established externally (for example, exclusively by a creator deity). The concept of predeterminism is often argued by invoking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Causal_determinism" title="Causal determinism">causal determinism</a>, implying that there is an unbroken <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chain_of_prior_occurrences" title="Chain of prior occurrences">chain of prior occurrences</a> stretching back to the origin of the universe. In the case of predeterminism, this chain of events has been pre-established, and human actions cannot interfere with the outcomes of this pre-established chain. Predeterminism can be used to mean such pre-established causal determinism, in which case it is categorised as a specific type of <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">determinism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKewan_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-McKewan-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> It can also be used interchangeably with causal determinism – in the context of its capacity to determine future events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McKewan_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-McKewan-93">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Despite this, predeterminism is often considered as independent of causal determinism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Society1971_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Society1971-98">[98]</a></sup> The term predeterminism is also frequently used in the context of biology and heredity, in which case it represents a form of <a href="/wiki/Biological_determinism" title="Biological determinism">biological determinism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>The term predeterminism suggests not just a determining of all events, but the prior and deliberately conscious determining of all events (therefore done, presumably, by a conscious being). While determinism usually refers to a naturalistically explainable causality of events, predeterminism seems by definition to suggest a person or a "someone" who is controlling or planning the causality of events before they occur and who then perhaps resides beyond the natural, causal universe. <a href="/wiki/Predestination" title="Predestination">Predestination</a> asserts that a supremely powerful being has indeed fixed all events and outcomes in the universe in advance, and is a famous doctrine of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calvinists" title="Calvinists">Calvinists</a> in <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">Christian theology</a>. Predestination is often considered a form of hard <a href="/wiki/Theological_determinism" title="Theological determinism">theological determinism</a>.
</p><p>Predeterminism has therefore been compared to <a href="/wiki/Fatalism" title="Fatalism">fatalism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Fatalism is the idea that everything is fated to happen, so that humans have no control over their future.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Theological_determinism">Theological determinism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Theological determinism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Theological_determinism" title="Theological determinism">Theological determinism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Theological_determinism" title="Theological determinism">Theological determinism</a> is a form of <a href="/wiki/Determinism" title="Determinism">determinism</a> stating that all events that happen are pre-ordained, or <a href="/wiki/Predestination" title="Predestination">predestined</a> to happen, by a <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">monotheistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a>, or that they are destined to occur given its <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">omniscience</a>. Two forms of theological determinism exist, here referenced as strong and weak theological determinism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JordanTate2004_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-JordanTate2004-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>The first one, strong theological determinism, is based on the concept of a <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">creator deity</a> dictating all events in history: "everything that happens has been predestined to happen by an omniscient, omnipotent divinity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iannone2001_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iannone2001-102">[102]</a></sup></li>
<li>The second form, weak theological determinism, is based on the concept of divine foreknowledge – "because <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">God</a>'s omniscience is perfect, what God knows about the future will inevitably happen, which means, consequently, that the future is already fixed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huyssteen2003_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Huyssteen2003-103">[103]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>There exist slight variations on the above categorisation. Some claim that theological determinism requires <a href="/wiki/Predestination" title="Predestination">predestination</a> of all events and outcomes by the divinity (that is, they do not classify the weaker version as 'theological determinism' unless libertarian free will is assumed to be denied as a consequence), or that the weaker version does not constitute 'theological determinism' at all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanArragon2010_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-VanArragon2010-54">[54]</a></sup> Theological determinism can also be seen as a form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Causal_determinism" title="Causal determinism">causal determinism</a>, in which the antecedent conditions are the nature and will of God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordmoralresponsibility_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordmoralresponsibility-55">[55]</a></sup> With respect to free will and the classification of theological compatibilism/incompatibilism below, "theological determinism is the thesis that God exists and has infallible knowledge of all true propositions including propositions about our future actions," more minimal criteria designed to encapsulate all forms of theological determinism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordincompatibilismarguments_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordincompatibilismarguments-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheologicalDeterminismXFreeWill.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="499" data-file-width="869" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/TheologicalDeterminismXFreeWill.svg/220px-TheologicalDeterminismXFreeWill.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/TheologicalDeterminismXFreeWill.svg/330px-TheologicalDeterminismXFreeWill.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/TheologicalDeterminismXFreeWill.svg/440px-TheologicalDeterminismXFreeWill.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheologicalDeterminismXFreeWill.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A simplified <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(general)" title="Taxonomy (general)">taxonomy</a> of philosophical positions regarding free will and theological determinism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordforeknowledge_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordforeknowledge-32">[32]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>There are various implications for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metaphysical_libertarianism" title="Metaphysical libertarianism">metaphysical libertarian</a> free will as consequent of theological determinism and its philosophical interpretation.
</p>
<ul><li>Strong theological determinism is not compatible with metaphysical libertarian free will, and is a form of <i>hard theological determinism</i> (equivalent to theological fatalism below). It claims that free will does not exist, and <i>God</i> has absolute control over a person's actions. Hard theological determinism is similar in implication to <a href="/wiki/Hard_determinism" title="Hard determinism">hard determinism</a>, although it does not invalidate <a href="/wiki/Compatibilism" title="Compatibilism">compatibilist</a> free will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordforeknowledge_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordforeknowledge-32">[32]</a></sup> Hard theological determinism is a form of theological incompatibilism (see figure, top left).</li>
<li>Weak theological determinism is either compatible or incompatible with metaphysical libertarian free will depending upon one's philosophical interpretation of <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">omniscience</a> – and as such is interpreted as either a form of hard theological determinism (known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theological_fatalism" title="Theological fatalism">theological fatalism</a>), or as <i>soft theological determinism</i> (terminology used for clarity only). Soft theological determinism claims that humans have free will to choose their actions, holding that God, while <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">knowing their actions before they happen</a>, does not affect the outcome. God's providence is "compatible" with voluntary choice. Soft theological determinism is known as theological compatibilism (see figure, top right). A rejection of theological determinism (or <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">divine foreknowledge</a>) is classified as theological incompatibilism also (see figure, bottom), and is relevant to a more general discussion of free will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordforeknowledge_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordforeknowledge-32">[32]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The basic argument for theological fatalism in the case of weak theological determinism is as follows:
</p>
<ol><li>Assume divine foreknowledge or <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">omniscience</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Infallibility" title="Infallibility">Infallible</a> foreknowledge implies destiny (it is known for certain what one will do)</li>
<li>Destiny eliminates alternate possibility (one cannot do otherwise)</li>
<li>Assert incompatibility with metaphysical libertarian free will</li></ol>
<p>This argument is very often accepted as a basis for theological incompatibilism: denying either libertarian free will or divine foreknowledge (omniscience) and therefore theological determinism. On the other hand, theological compatibilism must attempt to find problems with it. The formal version of the argument rests on a number of premises, many of which have received some degree of contention. Theological compatibilist responses have included:
</p>
<ul><li>Deny the truth value of <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_future_contingents" title="Problem of future contingents">future contingents</a>, although this denies foreknowledge and therefore theological determinism.</li>
<li>Assert differences in non-temporal knowledge (space-time independence), an approach taken for example by <a href="/wiki/Boethius" title="Boethius">Boethius</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C.S._Lewis" title="C.S. Lewis">C.S. Lewis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li>Deny the Principle of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Alternative_Possibilities&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Alternative Possibilities (page does not exist)">Alternate Possibilities</a>: "If you cannot do otherwise when you do an act, you do not act freely." For example, a human observer could in principle have a machine that could detect what will happen in the future, but the existence of this machine or their use of it has no influence on the outcomes of events.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zagzebski1991_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-zagzebski1991-107">[107]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>In the definition of <a href="/wiki/Compatibilism" title="Compatibilism">compatibilism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Incompatibilism" title="Incompatibilism">incompatibilism</a>, the literature often fails to distinguish between physical determinism and higher level forms of determinism (predeterminism, theological determinism, etc.) As such, hard determinism with respect to theological determinism (or "Hard Theological Determinism" above) might be classified as hard incompatibilism with respect to physical determinism (if no claim was made regarding the internal causality or determinism of the universe), or even compatibilism (if freedom from the constraint of determinism was not considered necessary for free will), if not hard determinism itself. By the same principle, metaphysical libertarianism (a form of incompatibilism with respect to physical determinism) might be classified as compatibilism with respect to theological determinism (if it was assumed such free will events were pre-ordained and therefore were destined to occur, but of which whose outcomes were not "predestined" or determined by God).
