If hard theological determinism is accepted (if it was assumed instead that such outcomes were predestined by God), then metaphysical libertarianism is not, however, possible, and would require reclassification (as hard incompatibilism for example, given that the universe is still assumed to be indeterministic – although the classification of hard determinism is technically valid also).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VanArragon2010_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-VanArragon2010-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Mind.E2.80.93body_problem"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Mind–body_problem">Mind–body problem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Mind–body problem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_problem" title="Mind–body problem">Mind–body problem</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">Philosophy of mind</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">Dualism (philosophy of mind)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">Monism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Physicalism" title="Physicalism">Physicalism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="817" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg/170px-Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg/255px-Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg/340px-Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Frans_Hals_-_Portret_van_Ren%C3%A9_Descartes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a></div></div></div>
<p>The idea of <i>free will</i> is one aspect of the <a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_problem" title="Mind–body problem">mind-body problem</a>, that is, consideration of the relation between <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> (for example, consciousness, memory, and judgment) and body (for example, the <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">human brain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">nervous system</a>). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_Mind" title="Philosophy of Mind">Philosophical models of mind</a> are divided into <a href="/wiki/Physicalism" title="Physicalism">physical</a> and non-physical expositions.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)#Substance_dualism" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">Cartesian dualism</a> holds that the mind is a nonphysical substance, the seat of consciousness and intelligence, and is not identical with physical states of the brain or body. It is suggested that although the two worlds do interact, each retains some measure of autonomy. Under cartesian dualism external mind is responsible for bodily action, although unconscious brain activity is often caused by external events (for example, the instantaneous reaction to being burned).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peruzzi_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Peruzzi-108">[108]</a></sup> Cartesian dualism implies that the physical world is not deterministic – and in which external mind controls (at least some) physical events, providing an interpretation of <a href="/wiki/Incompatibilism" title="Incompatibilism">incompatibilist</a> free will. Stemming from Cartesian dualism, a formulation sometimes called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)#Interactionism" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">interactionalist dualism</a></i> suggests a two-way interaction, that some physical events cause some mental acts and some mental acts cause some physical events. One modern vision of the possible separation of mind and body is the <a href="/wiki/Popper%27s_three_worlds" title="Popper's three worlds">"three-world" formulation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Popper</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Popper_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Popper-109">[109]</a></sup> Cartesian dualism and Popper's three worlds are two forms of what is called <a href="/wiki/Epistemological_pluralism" title="Epistemological pluralism">epistemological pluralism</a>, that is the notion that different epistemological methodologies are necessary to attain a full description of the world. Other forms of epistemological pluralist dualism include <a href="/wiki/Psychophysical_parallelism" title="Psychophysical parallelism">psychophysical parallelism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epiphenomenalism" title="Epiphenomenalism">epiphenomenalism</a>. Epistemological pluralism is one view in which the mind-body problem is <i>not</i> reducible to the concepts of the natural sciences.
</p><p>A contrasting approach is called <a href="/wiki/Physicalism" title="Physicalism">physicalism</a>. Physicalism is a <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_theory" title="Philosophical theory">philosophical theory</a> holding that everything that <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">exists</a> is no more extensive than its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_properties" title="Physical properties">physical properties</a>; that is, that there are no non-physical substances (for example physically independent minds). Physicalism can be reductive or non-reductive. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reductive_physicalism" title="Reductive physicalism">Reductive physicalism</a> is grounded in the idea that everything in the world can actually be reduced analytically to its fundamental physical, or material, basis. Alternatively, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-reductive_physicalism" title="Non-reductive physicalism">non-reductive physicalism</a> asserts that mental properties form a separate ontological class to physical properties: that mental states (such as <a href="/wiki/Qualia" title="Qualia">qualia</a>) are not ontologically reducible to physical states. Although one might suppose that mental states and neurological states are different in kind, that does not rule out the possibility that mental states are correlated with neurological states. In one such construction, <a href="/wiki/Anomalous_monism" title="Anomalous monism">anomalous monism</a>, mental events <a href="/wiki/Supervenience" title="Supervenience"><i>supervene</i></a> on physical events, describing the <a href="/wiki/Emergence" title="Emergence">emergence</a> of mental properties correlated with physical properties – implying causal reducibility. Non-reductive physicalism is therefore often categorised as <a href="/wiki/Property_dualism" title="Property dualism">property dualism</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">monism</a>, yet other types of property dualism do not adhere to the causal reducibility of mental states (see epiphenomenalism).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Incompatibilism" title="Incompatibilism">Incompatibilism</a> requires a distinction between the mental and the physical, being a commentary on the incompatibility of (determined) physical reality and one's presumably distinct experience of will. Secondarily, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metaphysical_libertarianism" title="Metaphysical libertarianism">metaphysical libertarian</a> free will must assert influence on physical reality, and where mind is responsible for such influence (as opposed to ordinary system randomness), it must be distinct from body to accomplish this. Both substance and property dualism offer such a distinction, and those particular models thereof that are not causally inert with respect to the physical world provide a basis for illustrating incompatibilist free will (i.e. interactionalist dualism and non-reductive physicalism).
</p><p>It has been noted that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_law" title="Physical law">laws of physics</a> have yet to resolve the <a href="/wiki/Hard_problem_of_consciousness" title="Hard problem of consciousness">hard problem of consciousness</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalat_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kalat-110">[110]</a></sup> "Solving the hard problem of consciousness involves determining how physiological processes such as ions flowing across the nerve membrane <i>cause</i> us to have experiences."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goldstein_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goldstein-111">[111]</a></sup> According to some, "Intricately related to the hard problem of consciousness, the hard problem of free will represents <i>the</i> core problem of conscious free will: Does conscious volition impact the material world?"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baumeister2_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Baumeister2-16">[16]</a></sup> Others however argue that "<a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a> plays a far smaller role in human life than Western culture has tended to believe."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-illusion_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-illusion-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Compatibilism">Compatibilism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Compatibilism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Compatibilism" title="Compatibilism">Compatibilism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Hobbes_(portrait).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1169" data-file-width="1109" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Thomas_Hobbes_%28portrait%29.jpg/170px-Thomas_Hobbes_%28portrait%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Thomas_Hobbes_%28portrait%29.jpg/255px-Thomas_Hobbes_%28portrait%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Thomas_Hobbes_%28portrait%29.jpg/340px-Thomas_Hobbes_%28portrait%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thomas_Hobbes_(portrait).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a> was a classical compatibilist.</div></div></div>
<p>Compatibilists maintain that determinism is compatible with free will. They believe freedom can be present or absent in a situation for reasons that have nothing to do with metaphysics. For instance, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Courts_of_law" title="Courts of law">courts of law</a> make judgments about whether individuals are acting under their own free will under certain circumstances without bringing in metaphysics. Similarly, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_liberty" title="Political liberty">political liberty</a> is a non-metaphysical concept.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Likewise, some compatibilists define free will as freedom to act according to one's determined motives without hindrance from other individuals. So for example Aristotle in his <i><a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> and the Stoic Chrysippus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>
In contrast, the <a href="/wiki/Incompatibilism" title="Incompatibilism">incompatibilist</a> positions are concerned with a sort of "metaphysically free will", which compatibilists claim has never been coherently defined. Compatibilists argue that determinism does not matter; though they disagree among themselves about what, in turn, <i>does</i> matter. To be a compatibilist, one need not endorse any particular conception of free will, but only deny that determinism is at odds with free will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CompSEP_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-CompSEP-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although there are various impediments to exercising one's choices, free will does not imply freedom of action. Freedom of choice (freedom to select one's will) is logically separate from freedom to <i>implement</i> that choice (freedom to enact one's will), although not all writers observe this distinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OConnor_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-OConnor-25">[25]</a></sup> Nonetheless, some philosophers have defined free will as the absence of various impediments. Some "modern compatibilists", such as <a href="/wiki/Harry_Frankfurt" title="Harry Frankfurt">Harry Frankfurt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a>, argue free will is simply freely choosing to do what constraints allow one to do. In other words, a coerced agent's choices can still be free if such coercion coincides with the agent's personal intentions and desires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DD1_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-DD1-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frankfurt_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Frankfurt-116">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_will_as_lack_of_physical_restraint">Free will as lack of physical restraint</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Free will as lack of physical restraint">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Most "classical compatibilists", such as <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes" title="Thomas Hobbes">Thomas Hobbes</a>, claim that a person is acting on the person's own will only when it is the desire of that person to do the act, and also possible for the person to be able to do otherwise, <i>if the person had decided to</i>. Hobbes sometimes attributes such compatibilist freedom to each individual and not to some abstract notion of <i>will</i>, asserting, for example, that "no liberty can be inferred to the will, desire, or inclination, but the liberty of the man; which consisteth in this, that he finds no stop, in doing what he has the will, desire, or inclination to doe [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>]."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hobbes_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hobbes-117">[117]</a></sup> In articulating this crucial proviso, <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> writes, "this hypothetical liberty is universally allowed to belong to every one who is not a prisoner and in chains."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hume_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hume-118">[118]</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a>, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Dictionnaire_philosophique" title="Dictionnaire philosophique">Dictionnaire philosophique</a></i>, claimed that "Liberty then is only and can be only the power to do what one will." He asked, "would you have everything at the pleasure of a million blind caprices?" For him, free will or liberty is "only the power of acting, what is this power? It is the effect of the constitution and present state of our organs."
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_will_as_a_psychological_state">Free will as a psychological state</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Free will as a psychological state">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Compatibilism often regards the agent free as virtue of their reason. Some explanations of free will focus on the internal causality of the mind with respect to higher-order brain processing – the interaction between conscious and unconscious brain activity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baumeister_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baumeister-119">[119]</a></sup> Likewise, some modern compatibilists in <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a> have tried to revive traditionally accepted struggles of free will with the formation of character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baumeister0_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baumeister0-120">[120]</a></sup> Compatibilist free will has also been attributed to our natural <a href="/wiki/Sense_of_agency" title="Sense of agency">sense of agency</a>, where one must believe they are an agent in order to function and develop a <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_mind" title="Theory of mind">theory of mind</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smilansky2000_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smilansky2000-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>The notion of levels of decision is presented in a different manner by Frankfurt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Frankfurt_116-1"><a href="#cite_note-Frankfurt-116">[116]</a></sup> Frankfurt argues for a version of compatibilism called the "hierarchical mesh". The idea is that an individual can have conflicting desires at a first-order level and also have a desire about the various first-order desires (a second-order desire) to the effect that one of the desires prevails over the others. A person's will is identified with their effective first-order desire, that is, the one they act on, and this will is free if it was the desire the person wanted to act upon, that is, the person's second-order desire was effective. So, for example, there are "wanton addicts", "unwilling addicts" and "willing addicts". All three groups may have the conflicting first-order desires to want to take the drug they are addicted to and to not want to take it.
</p><p>The first group, <i>wanton addicts</i>, have no second-order desire not to take the drug. The second group, "unwilling addicts", have a second-order desire not to take the drug, while the third group, "willing addicts", have a second-order desire to take it. According to Frankfurt, the members of the first group are devoid of will and therefore are no longer persons. The members of the second group freely desire not to take the drug, but their will is overcome by the addiction. Finally, the members of the third group willingly take the drug they are addicted to. Frankfurt's theory can ramify to any number of levels. Critics of the theory point out that there is no certainty that conflicts will not arise even at the higher-order levels of desire and preference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Others argue that Frankfurt offers no adequate explanation of how the various levels in the hierarchy mesh together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_will_as_unpredictability">Free will as unpredictability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Free will as unpredictability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In <i><a href="/wiki/Elbow_Room_(Dennett_book)" title="Elbow Room (Dennett book)">Elbow Room</a></i>, Dennett presents an argument for a compatibilist theory of free will, which he further elaborated in the book <i><a href="/wiki/Freedom_Evolves" title="Freedom Evolves">Freedom Evolves</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DD2_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-DD2-125">[125]</a></sup> The basic reasoning is that, if one excludes God, an infinitely powerful <a href="/wiki/Demon" title="Demon">demon</a>, and other such possibilities, then because of <a href="/wiki/Chaos_theory" title="Chaos theory">chaos</a> and epistemic limits on the precision of our knowledge of the current state of the world, the future is ill-defined for all finite beings. The only well-defined things are "expectations". The ability to do "otherwise" only makes sense when dealing with these expectations, and not with some unknown and unknowable future.
</p><p>According to Dennett, because individuals have the ability to act differently from what anyone expects, free will can exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DD2_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-DD2-125">[125]</a></sup> Incompatibilists claim the problem with this idea is that we may be mere "automata responding in predictable ways to stimuli in our environment". Therefore, all of our actions are controlled by forces outside ourselves, or by random chance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaney_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaney-126">[126]</a></sup> More sophisticated analyses of compatibilist free will have been offered, as have other critiques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CompSEP_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-CompSEP-115">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Decision_theory" title="Decision theory">decision theory</a>, a fundamental question is: From the standpoint of statistical outcomes, to what extent do the choices of a conscious being have the ability to influence the future? <a href="/wiki/Newcomb%27s_paradox" title="Newcomb's paradox">Newcomb's paradox</a> and other philosophical problems pose questions about free will and predictable outcomes of choices.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_physical_mind">The physical mind</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: The physical mind">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience_of_free_will" title="Neuroscience of free will">Neuroscience of free will</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compatibilist" title="Compatibilist">Compatibilist</a> models of free will often consider deterministic relationships as discoverable in the physical world (including the brain). Cognitive <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">naturalism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naturalism_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-naturalism-127">[127]</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Physicalism" title="Physicalism">physicalist</a> approach to studying human <a href="/wiki/Cognition" title="Cognition">cognition</a> and <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a> in which the mind is simply part of nature, perhaps merely a feature of many very complex self-programming feedback systems (for example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neural_networks" title="Neural networks">neural networks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_robotics" title="Cognitive robotics">cognitive robots</a>), and so must be studied by the methods of empirical science, such as the <a href="/wiki/Behavioural_sciences" title="Behavioural sciences">behavioral</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive sciences</a> (<i>i.e.</i> <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neuroscience</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_psychology" title="Cognitive psychology">cognitive psychology</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Peruzzi_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Peruzzi-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physicalism_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-physicalism-128">[128]</a></sup> Cognitive naturalism stresses the role of neurological sciences. Overall brain health, <a href="/wiki/Substance_dependence" title="Substance dependence">substance dependence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Long-term_depression" title="Long-term depression">depression</a>, and various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Personality_disorders" title="Personality disorders">personality disorders</a> clearly influence mental activity, and their impact upon <a href="/wiki/Volition_(psychology)" title="Volition (psychology)">volition</a> is also important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baumeister_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-Baumeister-119">[119]</a></sup> For example, an <a href="/wiki/Substance_abuse" title="Substance abuse">addict</a> may experience a conscious desire to escape addiction, but be unable to do so. The "will" is disconnected from the freedom to act. This situation is related to an abnormal production and distribution of <a href="/wiki/Dopamine" title="Dopamine">dopamine</a> in the brain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Volkow_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Volkow-129">[129]</a></sup> The neuroscience of free will places restrictions on both compatibilist and incompatibilist free will conceptions.
</p><p>Compatibilist models adhere to models of mind in which mental activity (such as deliberation) can be reduced to physical activity without any change in physical outcome. Although compatibilism is generally aligned to (or is at least compatible with) physicalism, some compatibilist models describe the natural occurrences of deterministic deliberation in the brain in terms of the first person perspective of the conscious agent performing the deliberation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baumeister2_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Baumeister2-16">[16]</a></sup> Such an approach has been considered a form of identity dualism. A description of "how conscious experience might affect brains" has been provided in which "the experience of conscious free will is the first-person perspective of the neural correlates of choosing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baumeister2_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-Baumeister2-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recently,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2018)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Claudio_Costa_(philosopher)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Claudio Costa (philosopher) (page does not exist)">Claudio Costa</a> developed a neocompatibilist theory based on the causal theory of action that is complementary to classical compatibilism. According to him, physical, psychological and rational restrictions can interfere at different levels of the causal chain that would naturally lead to action. Correspondingly, there can be physical restrictions to the body, psychological restrictions to the decision, and rational restrictions to the formation of reasons (desires plus beliefs) that should lead to what we would call a reasonable action. The last two are usually called "restrictions of free will". The restriction at the level of reasons is particularly important since it can be motivated by external reasons that are insufficiently conscious to the agent. One example was the collective suicide led by <a href="/wiki/Jim_Jones" title="Jim Jones">Jim Jones</a>. The suicidal agents were not conscious that their free will have been manipulated by external, even if ungrounded, reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-naturalism">Non-naturalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Non-naturalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Religious_naturalism" title="Religious naturalism">Religious naturalism</a>.</div>
<p>Alternatives to strictly <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">naturalist</a> physics, such as <a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_dualism" title="Mind–body dualism">mind–body dualism</a> positing a mind or soul existing apart from one's body while perceiving, thinking, choosing freely, and as a result acting independently on the body, include both traditional religious metaphysics and less common newer compatibilist concepts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mnn_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-mnn-131">[131]</a></sup> Also consistent with both autonomy and <a href="/wiki/Darwinism" title="Darwinism">Darwinism</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> they allow for free personal agency based on practical reasons within the laws of physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> While less popular among 21st-century philosophers, non-naturalist compatibilism is present in most if not almost all religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_views">Other views</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Other views">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some philosophers' views are difficult to categorize as either compatibilist or incompatibilist, hard determinist or libertarian. For example, <a href="/wiki/Ted_Honderich" title="Ted Honderich">Ted Honderich</a> holds the view that "determinism is true, compatibilism and incompatibilism are both false" and the real problem lies elsewhere. Honderich maintains that determinism is true because quantum phenomena are not events or things that can be located in space and time, but are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abstract_object" title="Abstract object">abstract</a> entities. Further, even if they were micro-level events, they do not seem to have any relevance to how the world is at the macroscopic level. He maintains that incompatibilism is false because, even if indeterminism is true, incompatibilists have not provided, and cannot provide, an adequate account of origination. He rejects compatibilism because it, like incompatibilism, assumes a single, fundamental notion of freedom. There are really two notions of freedom: voluntary action and origination. Both notions are required to explain freedom of will and responsibility. Both determinism and indeterminism are threats to such freedom. To abandon these notions of freedom would be to abandon moral responsibility. On the one side, we have our intuitions; on the other, the scientific facts. The "new" problem is how to resolve this conflict.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_will_as_an_illusion">Free will as an illusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Free will as an illusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spinoza.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1377" decoding="async" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Spinoza.jpg/170px-Spinoza.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Spinoza.jpg/255px-Spinoza.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Spinoza.jpg/340px-Spinoza.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spinoza.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spinoza thought that there is no free will.</div></div></div>
<dl><dd>"Experience teaches us no less clearly than reason, that men believe themselves free, simply because they are conscious of their actions, and unconscious of the causes whereby those actions are determined." <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Baruch Spinoza</a>, <i>Ethics</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spinoza_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spinoza-136">[136]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> discussed the possibility that the entire debate about free will is nothing more than a merely "verbal" issue. He suggested that it might be accounted for by "a false sensation or seeming experience" (a <i>velleity</i>), which is associated with many of our actions when we perform them. On reflection, we realize that they were necessary and determined all along.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Schopenhauer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="358" data-file-width="290" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Schopenhauer.jpg/170px-Schopenhauer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Schopenhauer.jpg/255px-Schopenhauer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8a/Schopenhauer.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Schopenhauer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Arthur Schopenhauer</a> claimed that phenomena do not have freedom of the will, but the will as <a href="/wiki/Noumenon" title="Noumenon">noumenon</a> is not subordinate to the laws of necessity (causality) and is thus free.</div></div></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Arthur Schopenhauer</a>, the actions of humans, as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenomena" title="Phenomena">phenomena</a>, are subject to the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_sufficient_reason" title="Principle of sufficient reason">principle of sufficient reason</a> and thus liable to necessity. Thus, he argues, humans do not possess free will as conventionally understood. However, the <a href="/wiki/Will_(philosophy)" title="Will (philosophy)">will</a> [urging, craving, striving, wanting, and desiring], as the <a href="/wiki/Noumenon" title="Noumenon">noumenon</a> underlying the phenomenal world, is in itself groundless: that is, not subject to time, space, and causality (the forms that governs the world of appearance). Thus, the will, in itself and outside of appearance, is free. Schopenhauer discussed the puzzle of free will and moral responsibility in <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_as_Will_and_Representation" title="The World as Will and Representation">The World as Will and Representation</a></i>, Book 2, Sec. 23:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>But the fact is overlooked that the individual, the person, is not will as <a href="/wiki/Thing-in-itself" title="Thing-in-itself">thing-in-itself</a>, but is <i>phenomenon</i> of the will, is as such determined, and has entered the form of the phenomenon, the principle of sufficient reason. Hence we get the strange fact that everyone considers himself to be <i>a priori</i> quite free, even in his individual actions, and imagines he can at any moment enter upon a different way of life… But <i>a posteriori</i> through experience, he finds to his astonishment that he is not free, but liable to necessity; that notwithstanding all his resolutions and reflections he does not change his conduct, and that from the beginning to the end of his life he must bear the same character that he himself condemns, and, as it were, must play to the end the part he has taken upon himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Schopenhauer elaborated on the topic in Book IV of the same work and in even greater depth in his later essay <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Freedom_of_the_Will" title="On the Freedom of the Will">On the Freedom of the Will</a>.</i> In this work, he stated, "You can do what you will, but in any given moment of your life you can <i>will</i> only one definite thing and absolutely nothing other than that one thing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his book <i><a href="/wiki/Free_Will_(book)" title="Free Will (book)">Free Will</a>,</i> philosopher and neuroscientist <a href="/wiki/Sam_Harris" title="Sam Harris">Sam Harris</a> argues that free will is an illusion, stating that "thoughts and intentions emerge from background causes of which we are unaware and over which we exert no conscious control."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Free_will_as_.22moral_imagination.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='Free_will_as_"moral_imagination"'>Free will as "moral imagination"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title='Edit section: Free will as "moral imagination"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Steiner" title="Rudolf Steiner">Rudolf Steiner</a>, who collaborated in a complete edition of Arthur Schopenhauer's work,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> wrote <i>The Philosophy of Freedom</i>, which focuses on the problem of free will. Steiner (1861–1925) initially divides this into the two aspects of freedom: <i>freedom of thought</i> and <i>freedom of action</i>. The controllable and uncontrollable aspects of decision making thereby are made logically separable, as pointed out in the introduction. This separation of <i>will</i> from <i>action</i> has a very long history, going back at least as far as <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">Stoicism</a> and the teachings of <a href="/wiki/Chrysippus" title="Chrysippus">Chrysippus</a> (279–206 BCE), who separated external <i>antecedent</i> causes from the internal disposition receiving this cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chrysippus_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chrysippus-142">[142]</a></sup>
</p><p>Steiner then argues that inner freedom is achieved when we integrate our sensory impressions, which reflect the outer appearance of the world, with our thoughts, which lend coherence to these impressions and thereby disclose to us an understandable world. Acknowledging the many influences on our choices, he nevertheless points out that they do not preclude freedom unless we fail to recognise them. Steiner argues that outer freedom is attained by permeating our deeds with <i>moral imagination.</i> "Moral" in this case refers to action that is willed, while "imagination" refers to the mental capacity to envision conditions that do not already hold. Both of these functions are necessarily conditions for freedom. Steiner aims to show that these two aspects of inner and outer freedom are integral to one another, and that true freedom is only achieved when they are united.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_will_as_a_pragmatically_useful_concept">Free will as a pragmatically useful concept</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Free will as a pragmatically useful concept">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/William_James" title="William James">William James</a>' views were ambivalent. While he believed in free will on "ethical grounds", he did not believe that there was evidence for it on scientific grounds, nor did his own introspections support it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Ultimately he believed that the problem of free will was a metaphysical issue and, therefore, could not be settled by science. Moreover, he did not accept incompatibilism as formulated below; he did not believe that the indeterminism of human actions was a prerequisite of moral responsibility. In his work <i><a href="/wiki/Pragmatism" title="Pragmatism">Pragmatism</a></i>, he wrote that "instinct and utility between them can safely be trusted to carry on the social business of punishment and praise" regardless of metaphysical theories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JW_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-JW-145">[145]</a></sup> He did believe that indeterminism is important as a "doctrine of relief" – it allows for the view that, although the world may be in many respects a bad place, it may, through individuals' actions, become a better one. Determinism, he argued, undermines <a href="/wiki/Meliorism" title="Meliorism">meliorism</a> – the idea that progress is a real concept leading to improvement in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JW_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-JW-145">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_will_and_views_of_causality">Free will and views of causality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Free will and views of causality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_sufficient_reason" title="Principle of sufficient reason">Principle of sufficient reason</a></div>
<p>In 1739, <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">David Hume</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/A_Treatise_of_Human_Nature" title="A Treatise of Human Nature">A Treatise of Human Nature</a></i> approached free will via the notion of causality. It was his position that causality was a mental construct used to explain the repeated association of events, and that one must examine more closely the relation between things <i>regularly succeeding</i> one another (descriptions of regularity in nature) and things that <i>result</i> in other things (things that cause or necessitate other things).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kane_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kane-146">[146]</a></sup> According to Hume, 'causation' is on weak grounds: "Once we realise that 'A must bring about B' is tantamount merely to 'Due to their constant conjunction, we are psychologically certain that B will follow A,' then we are left with a very weak notion of necessity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lorkowski_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lorkowski-147">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>This empiricist view was often denied by trying to prove the so-called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apriority" title="Apriority">apriority</a> of causal law (i.e. that it precedes all experience and is rooted in the construction of the perceivable world):
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Kant</a>'s proof in <i>Critique of Pure Reason</i> (which referenced time and time ordering of causes and effects)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arthur_Schopenhauer" title="Arthur Schopenhauer">Schopenhauer</a>'s proof from <i>The Fourfold Root of the Principle of Sufficient Reason</i> (which referenced the so-called intellectuality of representations, that is, in other words, objects and <a href="/wiki/Qualia" title="Qualia">qualia</a> perceived with senses)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>In the 1780s <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">Immanuel Kant</a> suggested at a minimum our decision processes with moral implications lie outside the reach of everyday causality, and lie outside the rules governing material objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hill-150">[150]</a></sup> "There is a sharp difference between moral judgments and judgments of fact… Moral judgments… must be <i>a priori</i> judgments."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paton_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paton-151">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>Freeman introduces what he calls "circular causality" to "allow for the contribution of self-organizing dynamics", the "formation of macroscopic population dynamics that shapes the patterns of activity of the contributing individuals", applicable to "interactions between neurons and neural masses… and between the behaving animal and its environment".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freeman_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freeman-152">[152]</a></sup> In this view, mind and neurological functions are tightly coupled in a situation where feedback between collective actions (mind) and individual subsystems (for example, <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">neurons</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Synapse" title="Synapse">synapses</a>) jointly decide upon the behaviour of both.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_will_according_to_Thomas_Aquinas">Free will according to Thomas Aquinas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Free will according to Thomas Aquinas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Thirteenth century philosopher <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">Thomas Aquinas</a> viewed humans as pre-programmed (by virtue of being human) to seek certain goals, but able to choose between routes to achieve these goals (our Aristotelian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telos_(philosophy)" title="Telos (philosophy)">telos</a>). His view has been associated with both compatibilism and libertarianism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>In facing choices, he argued that humans are governed by <i>intellect</i>, <i>will</i>, and <i>passions</i>. The will is "the primary mover of all the powers of the soul… and it is also the efficient cause of motion in the body."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stump_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stump-155">[155]</a></sup> Choice falls into five stages: (i) intellectual consideration of whether an objective is desirable, (ii) intellectual consideration of means of attaining the objective, (iii) will arrives at an intent to pursue the objective, (iv) will and intellect jointly decide upon choice of means (v) will elects execution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OConnor0_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-OConnor0-156">[156]</a></sup> Free will enters as follows: Free will is an "appetitive power", that is, not a cognitive power of intellect (the term "appetite" from Aquinas's definition "includes all forms of internal inclination").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> He states that judgment "concludes and terminates counsel. Now counsel is terminated, first, by the judgment of reason; secondly, by the acceptation of the appetite [that is, the free-will]."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>A compatibilist interpretation of Aquinas's view is defended thus: "Free-will is the cause of its own movement, because by his free-will man moves himself to act. But it does not of necessity belong to liberty that what is free should be the first cause of itself, as neither for one thing to be cause of another need it be the first cause. God, therefore, is the first cause, Who moves causes both natural and voluntary. And just as by moving natural causes He does not prevent their acts being natural, so by moving voluntary causes He does not deprive their actions of being voluntary: but rather is He the cause of this very thing in them; for He operates in each thing according to its own nature."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Free_will_as_a_pseudo-problem">Free will as a pseudo-problem</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Free will as a pseudo-problem">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Historically, most of the philosophical effort invested in resolving the dilemma has taken the form of close examination of definitions and ambiguities in the concepts designated by "free", "freedom", "will", "choice" and so forth. Defining 'free will' often revolves around the meaning of phrases like "ability to do otherwise" or "alternative possibilities". This emphasis upon words has led some philosophers to claim the problem is merely verbal and thus a pseudo-problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deery_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Deery-161">[161]</a></sup> In response, others point out the complexity of decision making and the importance of nuances in the terminology.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_philosophy">Eastern philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Eastern philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Buddhist_philosophy">Buddhist philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Buddhist philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> accepts both freedom and determinism (or something similar to it), but in spite of its focus towards the human <a href="/wiki/Agency_(philosophy)" title="Agency (philosophy)">agency</a>, rejects the western concept of a total agent from external sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gier_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gier-162">[162]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">the Buddha</a>, "There is free action, there is retribution, but I see no agent that passes out from one set of momentary elements into another one, except the [connection] of those elements."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gier_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gier-162">[162]</a></sup> Buddhists believe in neither absolute free will, nor determinism. It preaches a middle doctrine, named <i><a href="/wiki/Prat%C4%ABtyasamutp%C4%81da" title="Pratītyasamutpāda">pratītyasamutpāda</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>, often translated as "dependent origination", "dependent arising" or "conditioned genesis". It teaches that every volition is a conditioned action as a result of ignorance. In part, it states that free will is inherently conditioned and not "free" to begin with. It is also part of the theory of <a href="/wiki/Karma_in_Buddhism" title="Karma in Buddhism">karma in Buddhism</a>. The concept of karma in Buddhism is different from the notion of karma in Hinduism. In Buddhism, the idea of karma is much less deterministic. The Buddhist notion of karma is primarily focused on the cause and effect of moral actions in this life, while in Hinduism the concept of karma is more often connected with determining one's <a href="/wiki/Destiny" title="Destiny">destiny</a> in <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">future lives</a>.
</p><p>In Buddhism it is taught that the idea of absolute freedom of choice (that is that any human being could be completely free to make any choice) is unwise, because it denies the reality of one's physical needs and circumstances. Equally incorrect is the idea that humans have no choice in life or that their lives are pre-determined. To deny freedom would be to deny the efforts of Buddhists to make moral progress (through our capacity to freely choose compassionate action). <i>Pubbekatahetuvada</i>, the belief that all happiness and suffering arise from previous actions, is considered a wrong view according to Buddhist doctrines. Because Buddhists also reject agenthood, the traditional compatibilist strategies are closed to them as well. Instead, the Buddhist philosophical strategy is to examine the metaphysics of causality. Ancient India had many heated arguments about the nature of causality with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jain" title="Jain">Jains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nyaya" title="Nyaya">Nyayists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Samkhyists</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C%C4%81rv%C4%81ka" title="Cārvāka">Cārvākans</a>, and Buddhists all taking slightly different lines. In many ways, the Buddhist position is closer to a theory of "conditionality" (<i><a href="/wiki/Idappaccayat%C4%81" title="Idappaccayatā">idappaccayatā</a></i>) than a theory of "causality", especially as it is expounded by <a href="/wiki/Nagarjuna" title="Nagarjuna">Nagarjuna</a> in the <i><a href="/wiki/M%C5%ABlamadhyamakak%C4%81rik%C4%81" title="Mūlamadhyamakakārikā">Mūlamadhyamakakārikā</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gier_162-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gier-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hindu_philosophy">Hindu philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Hindu philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Free_will_in_theology#Hinduism" title="Free will in theology">Free will in theology § Hinduism</a></div>
<p>The six orthodox (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Astik" title="Astik">astika</a>) schools of thought in <a href="/wiki/Hindu_philosophy" title="Hindu philosophy">Hindu philosophy</a> do not agree with each other entirely on the question of free will. For the <a href="/wiki/Samkhya" title="Samkhya">Samkhya</a>, for instance, matter is without any freedom, and soul lacks any ability to control the unfolding of matter. The only real freedom (<i>kaivalya</i>) consists in realizing the ultimate separateness of matter and self.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> For the <a href="/wiki/Yoga" title="Yoga">Yoga</a> school, only <a href="/wiki/Ishvara" title="Ishvara">Ishvara</a> is truly free, and its freedom is also distinct from all feelings, thoughts, actions, or wills, and is thus not at all a freedom of will. The metaphysics of the <a href="/wiki/Nyaya" title="Nyaya">Nyaya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vaisheshika" title="Vaisheshika">Vaisheshika</a> schools strongly suggest a belief in determinism, but do not seem to make explicit claims about determinism or free will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-koller_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-koller-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>A quotation from <a href="/wiki/Swami_Vivekananda" title="Swami Vivekananda">Swami Vivekananda</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Vedanta" title="Vedanta">Vedantist</a>, offers a good example of the worry about free will in the Hindu tradition.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Therefore we see at once that there cannot be any such thing as free-will; the very words are a contradiction, because will is what we know, and everything that we know is within our universe, and everything within our universe is moulded by conditions of time, space and causality. ... To acquire freedom we have to get beyond the limitations of this universe; it cannot be found here.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-viveka_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-viveka-165">[165]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>However, the preceding quote has often been misinterpreted as Vivekananda implying that everything is predetermined. What Vivekananda actually meant by lack of free will was that the will was not "free" because it was heavily influenced by the law of cause and effect – "The will is not free, it is a phenomenon bound by cause and effect, but there is something behind the will which is free."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-viveka_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-viveka-165">[165]</a></sup> Vivekananda never said things were absolutely determined and placed emphasis on the power of conscious choice to alter one's past <a href="/wiki/Karma_in_Hinduism" title="Karma in Hinduism">karma</a>: "It is the coward and the fool who says this is his <a href="/wiki/Destiny" title="Destiny">fate</a>. But it is the strong man who stands up and says I will make my own fate."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-viveka_165-2"><a href="#cite_note-viveka-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_approaches">Scientific approaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Scientific approaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Science has contributed to the free will problem in at least three ways. First, physics has addressed the question of whether nature is deterministic, which is viewed as crucial by incompatibilists (compatibilists, however, view it as irrelevant). Second, although free will can be defined in various ways, all of them involve aspects of the way people make decisions and initiate actions, which have been studied extensively by neuroscientists. Some of the experimental observations are widely viewed as implying that free will does not exist or is an illusion (but many philosophers see this as a misunderstanding). Third, psychologists have studied the beliefs that the majority of ordinary people hold about free will and its role in assigning moral responsibility.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_physics">Quantum physics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Quantum physics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Early scientific thought often portrayed the universe as deterministic – for example in the thought of <a href="/wiki/Democritus" title="Democritus">Democritus</a> or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C%C4%81rv%C4%81ka" title="Cārvāka">Cārvākans</a> – and some thinkers claimed that the simple process of gathering sufficient information would allow them to predict future events with perfect accuracy. Modern science, on the other hand, is a mixture of deterministic and <a href="/wiki/Stochastic" title="Stochastic">stochastic</a> theories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">Quantum mechanics</a> predicts events only in terms of probabilities, casting doubt on whether the universe is deterministic at all, although evolution of the universal state vector is completely deterministic. Current physical theories cannot resolve the question of whether determinism is true of the world, being very far from a potential <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_everything" title="Theory of everything">theory of everything</a>, and open to many different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interpretation_of_quantum_mechanics" title="Interpretation of quantum mechanics">interpretations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stanfordcausaldeterminism_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-stanfordcausaldeterminism-167">[167]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>Assuming that an indeterministic interpretation of quantum mechanics is correct, one may still object that such indeterminism is for all practical purposes confined to microscopic phenomena.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> This is not always the case: many macroscopic phenomena are based on quantum effects. For instance, some <a href="/wiki/Hardware_random_number_generator" title="Hardware random number generator">hardware random number generators</a> work by amplifying quantum effects into practically usable signals. A more significant question is whether the indeterminism of quantum mechanics allows for the traditional idea of free will (based on a perception of free will). If a person's action is, however, only a result of complete quantum randomness, mental processes as experienced have no influence on the probabilistic outcomes (such as volition).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RKane1_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-RKane1-30">[30]</a></sup> According to many interpretations, non-determinism enables free will to exist,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> while others assert the opposite (because the action was not controllable by the physical being who claims to possess the free will).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Like physicists, <a href="/wiki/Biologist" title="Biologist">biologists</a> have frequently addressed questions related to free will. One of the most heated debates in biology is that of "<a href="/wiki/Nature_versus_nurture" title="Nature versus nurture">nature versus nurture</a>", concerning the relative importance of genetics and biology as compared to culture and environment in human behavior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pin_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pin-172">[172]</a></sup> The view of many researchers is that many human behaviors can be explained in terms of humans' brains, genes, and evolutionary histories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawk_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dawk-175">[175]</a></sup> This point of view raises the fear that such attribution makes it impossible to hold others responsible for their actions. <a href="/wiki/Steven_Pinker" title="Steven Pinker">Steven Pinker</a>'s view is that fear of determinism in the context of "genetics" and "evolution" is a mistake, that it is "a confusion of <i>explanation</i> with <i>exculpation</i>". Responsibility does not require that behavior be uncaused, as long as behavior responds to praise and blame.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pink_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pink-176">[176]</a></sup> Moreover, it is not certain that environmental determination is any less threatening to free will than genetic determination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lew_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lew-177">[177]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neuroscience_and_neurophilosophy">Neuroscience and neurophilosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Neuroscience and neurophilosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Neurophilosophy" title="Neurophilosophy">Neurophilosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience_of_free_will" title="Neuroscience of free will">Neuroscience of free will</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Neurostimulation" title="Neurostimulation">Neurostimulation</a></div>
<p>It has become possible to study the living <a href="/wiki/Human_brain" title="Human brain">brain</a>, and researchers can now watch the brain's decision-making process at work. A seminal experiment in this field was conducted by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Libet" title="Benjamin Libet">Benjamin Libet</a> in the 1980s, in which he asked each subject to choose a random moment to flick their wrist while he measured the associated activity in their brain; in particular, the build-up of electrical signal called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Readiness_potential" title="Readiness potential">readiness potential</a> (after German <a href="/wiki/Bereitschaftspotential" title="Bereitschaftspotential">Bereitschaftspotential</a>, which was discovered by <a href="/wiki/Hans_Helmut_Kornhuber" title="Hans Helmut Kornhuber">Kornhuber</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCder_Deecke" title="Lüder Deecke">Deecke</a> in 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup>). Although it was well known that the readiness potential reliably preceded the physical action, Libet asked whether it could be recorded before the conscious intention to move. To determine when subjects felt the intention to move, he asked them to watch the second hand of a clock. After making a movement, the volunteer reported the time on the clock when they first felt the conscious intention to move; this became known as Libet's W time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LGW_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-LGW-179">[179]</a></sup>
</p><p>Libet found that the <i>unconscious</i> brain activity of the readiness potential leading up to subjects' movements began approximately half a second before the subject was aware of a conscious intention to move.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LGW_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-LGW-179">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>These studies of the timing between actions and the conscious decision bear upon the role of the brain in understanding free will. A subject's declaration of intention to move a finger appears <i>after</i> the brain has begun to implement the action, suggesting to some that unconsciously the brain has made the decision <i>before</i> the conscious mental act to do so. Some believe the implication is that free will was not involved in the decision and is an illusion. The first of these experiments reported the brain registered activity related to the move about 0.2 s before movement onset.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Libet_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Libet-181">[181]</a></sup> However, these authors also found that awareness of action was <i>anticipatory</i> to activity in the muscle underlying the movement; the entire process resulting in action involves more steps than just the <i>onset</i> of brain activity. The bearing of these results upon notions of free will appears complex.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strother_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strother-182">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rosenbaum_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rosenbaum-183">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some argue that placing the question of free will in the context of motor control is too narrow. The objection is that the time scales involved in motor control are very short, and motor control involves a great deal of unconscious action, with much physical movement entirely unconscious. On that basis "…free will cannot be squeezed into time frames of 150–350 <a href="/wiki/Millisecond" title="Millisecond">ms</a>; free will is a longer term phenomenon" and free will is a higher level activity that "cannot be captured in a description of neural activity or of muscle activation…"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gallagher_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gallagher-184">[184]</a></sup> The bearing of timing experiments upon free will is still under discussion.
</p><p>More studies have since been conducted, including some that try to:
</p>
<ul><li>support Libet's original findings</li>
<li>suggest that the cancelling or "veto" of an action may first arise subconsciously as well</li>
<li>explain the underlying brain structures involved</li>
<li>suggest models that explain the relationship between conscious intention and action</li></ul>
<p>Benjamin Libet's results are quoted<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> in favor of epiphenomenalism, but he believes subjects still have a "conscious veto", since the readiness potential does not invariably lead to an action. In <i><a href="/wiki/Freedom_Evolves" title="Freedom Evolves">Freedom Evolves</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a> argues that a no-free-will conclusion is based on dubious assumptions about the location of consciousness, as well as questioning the accuracy and interpretation of Libet's results. Kornhuber and Deecke underlined that absence of conscious will during the early Bereitschaftspotential (termed BP1) is not a proof of the non-existence of free will, as also unconscious agendas may be free and non-deterministic. According to their suggestion, man has relative freedom, i.e. freedom in degrees, that can be increased or decreased through deliberate choices that involve both conscious and unconscious (panencephalic) processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup>
</p><p>Others have argued that data such as the Bereitschaftspotential undermine epiphenomenalism for the same reason, that such experiments rely on a subject reporting the point in time at which a conscious experience occurs, thus relying on the subject to be able to consciously perform an action. That ability would seem to be at odds with early epiphenomenalism, which according to Huxley is the broad claim that consciousness is "completely without any power… as the steam-whistle which accompanies the work of a locomotive engine is without influence upon its machinery".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flanagan1992_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flanagan1992-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>Adrian G. Guggisberg and Annaïs Mottaz have also challenged those findings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>A study by Aaron Schurger and colleagues published in the Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> challenged assumptions about the causal nature of the readiness potential itself (and the "pre-movement buildup" of neural activity in general), casting doubt on conclusions drawn from studies such as Libet's<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LGW_179-2"><a href="#cite_note-LGW-179">[179]</a></sup> and Fried's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fried_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fried-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p><p>A study that compared deliberate and arbitrary decisions, found that the early signs of decision are absent for the deliberate ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has been shown that in several brain-related conditions, individuals cannot entirely control their own actions, though the existence of such conditions does not directly refute the existence of free will. Neuroscientific studies are valuable tools in developing models of how humans experience free will.
</p><p>For example, people with <a href="/wiki/Tourette_syndrome" title="Tourette syndrome">Tourette syndrome</a> and related <a href="/wiki/Tic_disorder" title="Tic disorder">tic disorders</a> make involuntary movements and utterances (called <a href="/wiki/Tic" title="Tic">tics</a>) despite the fact that they would prefer not to do so when it is socially inappropriate. Tics are described as semi-voluntary or <i>unvoluntary</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TSADef_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-TSADef-192">[192]</a></sup> because they are not strictly <i>involuntary</i>: they may be experienced as a <i>voluntary</i> response to an unwanted, premonitory urge. Tics are experienced as irresistible and must eventually be expressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TSADef_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-TSADef-192">[192]</a></sup> People with Tourette syndrome are sometimes able to suppress their tics for limited periods, but doing so often results in an explosion of tics afterward. The control exerted (from seconds to hours at a time) may merely postpone and exacerbate the ultimate expression of the tic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zinner_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zinner-193">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Alien_hand_syndrome" title="Alien hand syndrome">alien hand syndrome</a>, the affected individual's limb will produce unintentional movements without the will of the person. The affected limb effectively demonstrates 'a will of its own.' The <a href="/wiki/Sense_of_agency" title="Sense of agency">sense of agency</a> does not emerge in conjunction with the overt appearance of the purposeful act even though the sense of ownership in relationship to the body part is maintained. This phenomenon corresponds with an impairment in the premotor mechanism manifested temporally by the appearance of the readiness potential recordable on the scalp several hundred milliseconds before the overt appearance of a spontaneous willed movement. Using <a href="/wiki/Functional_magnetic_resonance_imaging" title="Functional magnetic resonance imaging">functional magnetic resonance imaging</a> with specialized multivariate analyses to study the temporal dimension in the activation of the cortical network associated with voluntary movement in human subjects, an anterior-to-posterior sequential activation process beginning in the supplementary motor area on the medial surface of the frontal lobe and progressing to the primary motor cortex and then to parietal cortex has been observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kayser_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kayser-194">[194]</a></sup> The sense of agency thus appears to normally emerge in conjunction with this orderly sequential network activation incorporating premotor association cortices together with primary motor cortex. In particular, the supplementary motor complex on the medial surface of the frontal lobe appears to activate prior to primary motor cortex presumably in associated with a preparatory pre-movement process. In a recent study using functional magnetic resonance imaging, alien movements were characterized by a relatively isolated activation of the primary motor cortex contralateral to the alien hand, while voluntary movements of the same body part included the natural activation of motor association cortex associated with the premotor process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Assal_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Assal-195">[195]</a></sup> The clinical definition requires "feeling that one limb is foreign or has a <i>will of its own,</i> together with observable involuntary motor activity" (emphasis in original).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Doody_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-Doody-196">[196]</a></sup> This syndrome is often a result of damage to the <a href="/wiki/Corpus_callosum" title="Corpus callosum">corpus callosum</a>, either when it is severed to treat intractable <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a> or due to a <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a>. The standard neurological explanation is that the felt will reported by the speaking left hemisphere does not correspond with the actions performed by the non-speaking right hemisphere, thus suggesting that the two hemispheres may have independent senses of will.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scepkowski_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scepkowski-197">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bundick_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bundick-198">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, one of the most important ("first rank") diagnostic symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a> is the patient's delusion of being controlled by an external force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneider_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schneider-199">[199]</a></sup> People with schizophrenia will sometimes report that, although they are acting in the world, they do not recall initiating the particular actions they performed. This is sometimes likened to being a robot controlled by someone else. Although the neural mechanisms of schizophrenia are not yet clear, one influential hypothesis is that there is a breakdown in brain systems that compare motor commands with the feedback received from the body (known as <a href="/wiki/Proprioception" title="Proprioception">proprioception</a>), leading to attendant <a href="/wiki/Hallucination" title="Hallucination">hallucinations</a> and delusions of control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-frith_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-frith-200">[200]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Experimental_psychology">Experimental psychology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Free_will&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Experimental psychology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">Cognitive science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_psychology" title="Cognitive psychology">Cognitive psychology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">Neuroscience</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Experimental_psychology" title="Experimental psychology">Experimental psychology</a>'s contributions to the free will debate have come primarily through social psychologist <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Wegner" title="Daniel Wegner">Daniel Wegner</a>'s work on conscious will.
